Figure 2. Taxonomy of representative automatic evaluation methods in NLG. The detailed classifications about LLM-based evaluation methods could be found in Section [3](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#S3 "3. Automatic Evaluation for Neural Text Generation (NLG) ‣ A Survey of Automatic Evaluation Methods on Text, Visual and Speech Generations").

In this section, we comprehensively review the progress of automatic evaluation techniques for Neural Language Generation (NLG) tasks, including open-ended text generation, summarization, translation, etc. Figure 2 presents a comprehensive taxonomy of automatic evaluation methods for NLG tasks, which can be broadly classified into five main categories: (1) Heuristic evaluation uses rule-based and heuristic features in natural language for assessment, such as word-overlap metrics or generation probabilities; (2) Embedding-based evaluation measures semantic similarity between references and generated texts (Zhang et al., [2020](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib486)); (3) Learning-based evaluation trains neural networks on human-annotated data to assess text quality; (4) LLM-based evaluation employs large language models to perform evaluations through carefully designed prompts and chain-of-thought reasoning, also known as LLM-as-a-judge (Li et al., [2024b](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib181)); and (5) Benchmark-based evaluation tests specific capabilities using human-annotated benchmarks, such as mathematical reasoning and code generation. The following subsections provide detailed explorations of each category with their representative works. Finally, in Section [3.5](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#S3.SS5 "3.5. Comparing Automatic Evaluations ‣ 3. Automatic Evaluation for Neural Text Generation (NLG) ‣ A Survey of Automatic Evaluation Methods on Text, Visual and Speech Generations"), we systematically compare and analyze the characteristics of these automated evaluation paradigms.

### 3.1. Heuristic Evaluation

Heuristic evaluation relies on rule-based or heuristic features for evaluation. Existing heuristic methods could be classified into three categories: (1) Word-Overlap; (2) Edit-Distance; and (3) Generation Probability. Table [1](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#S3.T1 "Table 1 ‣ 3.1.4. Other Heuristic Attributions ‣ 3.1. Heuristic Evaluation ‣ 3. Automatic Evaluation for Neural Text Generation (NLG) ‣ A Survey of Automatic Evaluation Methods on Text, Visual and Speech Generations") provides a complete overview of these methods.

#### 3.1.1. Word-overlap

Word-overlap-based heuristic metrics evaluate text generation through three main criteria:

##### (1) Text Similarity:

These metrics assume that high-quality generated texts should closely match ground-truth texts (Ren et al., [2020](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib314); Hardy et al., [2019](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib96); Dhingra et al., [2019](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib60)). Common examples include BLEU (Papineni et al., [2002](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib284)), ROUGE (Lin, [2004](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib203)), and METEOR (Banerjee and Lavie, [2005](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib14)), which measure machine translation and summarization quality using $`n`$-gram overlap (precision and recall). Additionally, chrF (Popović, [2015](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib297)) and METEOR (Banerjee and Lavie, [2005](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib14)) combine precision and recall in $`n`$-gram matching to provide more accurate similarity assessments. NIST (Doddington, [2002](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib61)) and CIDEr (Vedantam et al., [2015](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib386)) assign weights to different $`n`$-grams, focusing on more important words and phrases.

##### (2) Text Diversity:

Diversity is crucial for open-domain text generation. Metrics such as Rep-$`n`$ (Su and Collier, [2023](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib349)) and Distinct-$`N`$ (Li et al., [2016](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib185)) measure generation quality by calculating the proportion of unique $`n`$-grams in the output. Self-BLEU (Zhu et al., [2018](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib509)) calculates the BLEU score for each generated sentence and uses the average score to measure diversity. To address Distinct-$`N`$’s bias toward longer texts, Expectation-Adjusted Distinct (EAD) (Liu et al., [2022](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib219)) adjusts the number of distinct tokens based on statistical expectations.

##### (3) Factual Consistency:

Factual consistency or faithfulness is essential for text summarization. Most automatic evaluation methods use question answering (QA) models (Rajpurkar et al., [2018](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib308)) to generate answers from both the summary and source document. Word-overlap metrics then measure the similarity between these answers (Durmus et al., [2020](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib63); Scialom et al., [2021](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib332); Wang et al., [2020](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib393)).

Most word-overlap-based metrics require references (reference-based), as they measure differences between generated text and reference text. Although HighRES (Hardy et al., [2019](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib96)) is reference-free, it still needs human-annotated source sentences from documents to serve as reference proxies for evaluating generated summaries.

#### 3.1.2. Edit-Distance

Unlike word-overlap-based methods that compute $`n`$-gram overlap, edit-distance-based methods evaluate text quality by counting the number of character or word-level transformations needed to convert generated text into reference text (Moramarco et al., [2022](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib266)). TER (Snover et al., [2006](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib341)) and WER (Negri et al., [2014](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib273)) are two representative methods that evaluate the quality of model-generated summaries and translations by computing edit distance from ground-truth.

#### 3.1.3. Probability-based

While word-overlap and edit-distance methods use $`n`$-gram lexical features, many approaches leverage probability features for evaluation (Moon et al., [2022](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib265); Ke et al., [2022](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib141); Su and Collier, [2023](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib349)), such as perplexity (PPL) (Jelinek et al., [1977](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib127)). These methods assume that generations with high probability have better quality. Pre-trained Language Models (PLMs) such as BERT (Devlin et al., [2019](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib59)) and BART (Lewis et al., [2020](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib179)) are commonly used to compute generation probability. For example, BARTScore (Yuan et al., [2021](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib458)) measures generation probability using pre-trained language models given the input or reference. Additionally, CTRLEval (Ke et al., [2022](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib141)) evaluates text generation across multiple dimensions—coherence, consistency, and attribute relevance—based on context (prefix and attribute label) by computing generation probability in designed text infilling tasks. An increasing number of studies now use large language models (LLMs) to evaluate generated text probability (Li et al., [2024f](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib195)), such as GPTScore (Fu et al., [2023a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib72)). Though these approaches share similarities with other probability-based methods, we discuss them separately under the LLM-based evaluation paradigm (Section [3.4](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#S3.SS4 "3.4. LLM-based Evaluation ‣ 3. Automatic Evaluation for Neural Text Generation (NLG) ‣ A Survey of Automatic Evaluation Methods on Text, Visual and Speech Generations")) due to their specific use of LLMs.

#### 3.1.4. Other Heuristic Attributions

Beyond word-overlap, edit-distance, and generation probability, several approaches leverage alternative heuristic features for evaluation. For example, FACE (Yang et al., [2023](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib444)) measures text similarity based on Fourier Analysis of estimated Cross-Entropy of text. Some methods focus on corpus-level evaluation. Mark-Evaluate (Mordido and Meinel, [2020](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib267)) introduces evaluation metrics inspired by population size estimators widely used in ecology. Zipf (Holtzman et al., [2020](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib107)) measures the exponential relationship between word rank and frequency in text, analyzing how well generated text adheres to natural language distribution patterns. For faithfulness evaluation, Q² (Honovich et al., [2021](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib108)) uses Natural Language Inference (NLI) models to compare answers generated with knowledge sources to answers generated from dialogue responses, quantifying information consistency between sources and generations.

[TABLE]

Table 1. The representative evaluation methods for heuristic methods. Based on whether references are required, the methods can be further divided into reference-based and reference-free. The same principle applies to the context dimension (context-free and context-based).

### 3.2. Embedding-based Evaluation

Heuristic evaluation methods often overlook semantic features in text generation, which leads to significant discrepancies between automated and human evaluation results. With the rapid development of distributed word embeddings and representation learning techniques (Tsann et al., [2022](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib375); Pennington et al., [2014](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib292)), Embedding-based Evaluation has emerged as an alternative approach that measures the semantic similarity between generated text (Zhang et al., [2020](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib486)) and the context or reference. Based on the methods used to measure embedding similarity, existing embedding-based evaluation approaches can be categorized into three main types: (1) Cosine Similarity; (2) Embedding Distance; and (3) Divergence. Table [2](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#S3.T2 "Table 2 ‣ 3.2. Embedding-based Evaluation ‣ 3. Automatic Evaluation for Neural Text Generation (NLG) ‣ A Survey of Automatic Evaluation Methods on Text, Visual and Speech Generations") summarizes the current embedding-based evaluation metrics, which we discuss in detail below.

[TABLE]

Table 2. The complete list of the embedding-based evaluation methods.

#### 3.2.1. Cosine similarity

Cosine similarity is the most common approach for measuring semantic similarity between two sentence embeddings (Zhao et al., [2023](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib496); Lo, [2019](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib232); Mathur et al., [2019](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib254); Zhan et al., [2021](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib473); Gao et al., [2020c](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib80); Chen and Guo, [2015](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib25)). Before the advent of pre-trained language models like BERT (Devlin et al., [2019](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib59)), researchers typically used pre-trained word embeddings such as Word2Vec (Mikolov et al., [2013](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib259)) and GloVe (Pennington et al., [2014](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib292)) to construct semantic vectors for text (Ng and Abrecht, [2015](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib275); Sun and Nenkova, [2019](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib355)). For example, Vector Extrema, Embedding Average, and Greedy Matching (Liu et al., [2016](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib213)) apply cosine similarity to evaluate dialog generation quality based on the pre-trained word embeddings. With the emergence of pre-trained language models (PLMs) such as BERT (Devlin et al., [2019](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib59)) and RoBERTa (Liu et al., [2020](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib225)), semantic embeddings from these models have demonstrated superior ability to capture semantic information, leading to more robust evaluation methods (Chen et al., [2021a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib30); Zhao et al., [2020](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib494); Li et al., [2021](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib196); Zhu et al., [2023b](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib508); Zhou et al., [2023a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib505)). A notable example is BERTScore (Zhang\* et al., [2020](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib487)), a representative embedding-based metric that extracts semantic vectors using BERT and computes the IDF-weighted maximum cosine similarity between each token pair from reference and generated text.

#### 3.2.2. Word Mover’s Distance (WMD)

WMD calculates the minimum transport cost (distance) between two sets of word embeddings(Johnson, [2022](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib133)), with Euclidean distance and $`\mathcal{L}_{p}`$ distance (where $`p \in {1,2,{+ \infty}}`$) being the most frequently used measures (Colombo et al., [2021b](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib45)). MoverScore (Zhao et al., [2019](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib495)), for instance, uses Euclidean distance as the transport cost to compute WMD between generated and reference texts using BERT word embeddings. Similarly, WMD (Kusner et al., [2015](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib158)) and Sentence Mover’s Similarity (SMS) (Clark et al., [2019](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib40)) measure the cost of transforming generated text into reference text using semantic embeddings. Besides, Xiang et al. ([2021](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib432)) introduced Frechet Bert Distance (FBD) to compute the distance between references and generated texts.

#### 3.2.3. Divergence

Divergence represents another category of embedding-based evaluation metrics. InfoLM (Colombo et al., [2021a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib44)) computes three families of divergences on discrete probability distributions over the vocabulary generated by PLMs. MAUVE (Pillutla et al., [2021](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib295)) measures the KL-divergence between reference and generated texts by analyzing their mixture distributions.

### 3.3. Learning-based Evaluation

Embedding-based evaluation methods often show limited correlation with human judgments due to two key limitations: (1) they operate at a coarse-grained level, failing to capture critical error patterns, especially in grammar and diversity; and (2) they rely on finite reference sets that inadequately represent the full spectrum of high-quality responses, potentially penalizing valid generations that differ from references. Inspired by data-driven machine learning approaches, Learning-based Evaluation uses deep neural networks to mimic human evaluation patterns (Lowe et al., [2017](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib234)) by training on specially constructed evaluation datasets. The training dataset forms the core of learning-based evaluation methods. Current approaches can be classified into three categories based on their dataset construction methods: (1) Supervised methods train evaluation models on human-annotated datasets; (2) Self-supervised methods train evaluation models on synthetic datasets generated through heuristic strategies; (3) Mixture methods employ a two-stage training approach, typically pre-training on large-scale synthetic data followed by fine-tuning on human-annotated datasets.

[TABLE]

Table 3. The representative learning-based (supervised learning) evaluation methods.

#### 3.3.1. Supervised

Supervised learning-based automatic evaluation methods train models to mimic human assessment using high-quality human-annotated datasets (Mathur et al., [2019](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib254); Zhang et al., [2021b](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib475); Weston et al., [2022](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib422); Liu et al., [2023a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib222); Pu et al., [2021](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib299); Rubino et al., [2021](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib320); Bulian et al., [2022](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib18); Wang et al., [2022](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib408)). Table [3](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#S3.T3 "Table 3 ‣ 3.3. Learning-based Evaluation ‣ 3. Automatic Evaluation for Neural Text Generation (NLG) ‣ A Survey of Automatic Evaluation Methods on Text, Visual and Speech Generations") provides a complete list of these methods, with representative approaches described below.

##### (1) Reference-based and Context-free Methods:

Early reference-based and context-free supervised methods typically fine-tuned hyper-parameters or trained pairwise classifiers or regression models based on RNN/LSTM architectures (Guzmán et al., [2015](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib94), [2014](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib93); Gupta et al., [2015](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib92)). For instance, Q-BLEU4 (Nema and Khapra, [2018](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib274)) tunes two hyper-parameters on human evaluation scores, combining answerability scores with BLEU-4 scores for automatic question generation (AQG) systems. RUSE (Shimanaka et al., [2018](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib340)) implements a regression model trained directly on human-rated translation quality scores.

##### (2) Reference-based and Context-based Methods:

These methods evaluate generated text by considering both references and contextual information. A notable example is COMET (Rei et al., [2020](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib312)), which offers two variants—COMET-MQM and COMET-DARR—trained on human-evaluated machine translation corpora that incorporate source sentences and reference translations. Similarly, Takahashi et al. ([2020](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib362)) developed machine translation evaluation methods based on the XLM model (Lample and Conneau, [2019](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib166)). This approach extends to other natural language generation tasks as well. For text simplification, LENS (Maddela et al., [2023](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib246)) outperforms strong baselines like BERTScore by training on human-evaluated simplified texts. RADE (Shi et al., [2023](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib339)) assesses dialog generation quality using human responses as references. Additionally, LERC (Chen et al., [2020](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib24)) trains on human judgments from its MOCHA dataset for generative reading comprehension tasks, while ADEM (Lowe et al., [2017](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib234)) optimizes RNN models to evaluate dialog utterance quality based on conversation history and reference responses.

##### (3) Reference-free and Context-based Methods:

These methods are widely used for open-domain generation tasks, where outputs that differ from limited reference examples may still be of high quality. In such cases, only contextual information is useful for evaluating generation quality. For example, DialogRPT (Gao et al., [2020a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib77)) fine-tunes the DialoGPT model (Zhang et al., [2020a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib491)) on human-rated datasets to create a ranking-based evaluation metric. DEB (Sai et al., [2020b](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib325)) builds a high-quality human-annotated dataset specifically for training dialogue response evaluation models, incorporating multiple references and adversarial responses. Additionally, several approaches focus on evaluating the faithfulness of generated content relative to its context (Fabbri et al., [2022](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib66); Laban et al., [2022b](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib162); Zha et al., [2023a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib471)). QAFactEval (Fabbri et al., [2022](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib66)) and SCConv (Laban et al., [2022b](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib162)) train models to predict faithfulness scores using sentence-level natural language inference (NLI) scores as feature vectors. AlignScore (Zha et al., [2023a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib471)) employs a more comprehensive approach by pre-training its evaluation model on seven established tasks: NLI, question answering, paraphrasing, fact verification, information retrieval, semantic similarity, and summarization.

##### (4) Reference-free and Context-free Supervised Methods:

This category represents a specialized type of supervised learning-based evaluation that primarily assesses the inherent quality of generated text without relying on references or context. Examples include StoryER (Chen et al., [2022a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib27)) and ASE-Eval (Taghipour and Ng, [2016](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib361)), which are designed for text simplification, story generation, and Automated Essay Scoring (ASE), respectively. These applications typically operate with minimal contextual constraints, focusing instead on intrinsic quality measures of the generated content.

#### 3.3.2. Self-supervised Learning Approaches

Due to the substantial resources required for creating human-annotated datasets, researchers have developed various self-supervised strategies to construct evaluation datasets automatically. These self-supervised approaches can be classified into four main categories: (1) text perturbation techniques, which introduce controlled modifications to existing texts; (2) negative sampling methods, which sample negative samples from existing text corpus; (3) generative augmentation approaches, which utilizes language models to generate samples; and (4) positive sample augmentation strategies, which generate positive samples to balance the training dataset distribution. A comprehensive overview of these self-supervised learning-based evaluation methods is presented in Table [4](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#S3.T4 "Table 4 ‣ 3.3.2. Self-supervised Learning Approaches ‣ 3.3. Learning-based Evaluation ‣ 3. Automatic Evaluation for Neural Text Generation (NLG) ‣ A Survey of Automatic Evaluation Methods on Text, Visual and Speech Generations").

[TABLE]

Table 4. The complete list of the self-supervised learning-based evaluation methods.

##### (1) Text Perturbation:

This widely-adopted strategy generates negative samples through various text modification operations, including repetition (Park et al., [2023](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib285)), insertion (Wu et al., [2023a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib425)), deletion (Zhong et al., [2022](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib501); Guan and Huang, [2020](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib88)), substitution (Guan and Huang, [2020](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib88)), reordering (Wu et al., [2020](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib426)), back-translation (Zhang et al., [2022a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib476)), and mask-and-fill (Zhang et al., [2022a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib476)). For example, DENSITY (Park et al., [2023](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib285)), ReFreeEval (Wu et al., [2023a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib425)), LS Score (Wu et al., [2020](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib426)), and UniEval (Zhong et al., [2022](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib501)) apply random-span repetition, insertion, deletion, substitution, and reordering operations to generate low-quality text with intentional flaws. UNION (Guan and Huang, [2020](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib88)) introduces negation alteration specifically for story generation tasks. Additionally, MDD-Eval (Zhang et al., [2022a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib476)) and PoE (Zhang et al., [2023a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib478)) use back-translation and mask-and-fill operations to generate text that appears fluent but contains subtle flaws. Recently, BCR (Yuan et al., [2023](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib455)) constructed medium-coherence negative responses by shuffling utterances from one speaker in dialogues.

##### (2) Negative Sampling:

This strategy samples negative examples from large-scale corpora (Mehri and Eskenazi, [2020a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib256); Ghazarian et al., [2019](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib82)). For instance, RUBER (Tao et al., [2017](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib368)) randomly samples dialogue utterances from a corpus to create negative examples for each user query. Since random negative samples are usually easily distinguishable, many researchers have developed hard negative sampling strategies (Xu et al., [2023a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib437); Zhang et al., [2022b](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib477), [2021a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib474); Zhou and Xu, [2020](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib506); Zhang et al., [2021c](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib479)). For example, PoNe (Lan et al., [2020](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib168)) and GRADE (Huang et al., [2020](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib117)) select context-similar and reference-similar generations as challenging negative samples.

##### (3) Generative Augmentation:

This approach uses pre-trained language models (PLMs) to generate text that is linguistically fluent but contextually inappropriate. MDD-Eval demonstrates this by employing DialoGPT (Zhang et al., [2020b](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib492)) to create such samples.

##### (4) Positive Sample Augmentation:

Unlike the previous methods that focus primarily on collecting negative samples, this approach aims to balance datasets by adding positive examples (Zhang et al., [2022a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib476)). Both PoNe (Lan et al., [2020](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib168)) and PoE (Zhang et al., [2023a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib478)) implement this strategy.

Despite the efficiency of self-supervised methods in generating training data, they often struggle with noise in the form of false negatives. For instance, generative augmentation and hard negative sampling methods sometimes inadvertently include appropriate generations in the negative sample set. To address this challenge, several works (Lan et al., [2020](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib168); Zhang et al., [2022a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib476), [2023a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib478)) have developed pseudo-labeling techniques to identify and remove these false negatives. PoNe (Lan et al., [2020](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib168)), for example, implements an iterative optimization algorithm specifically designed to detect such problematic samples.

#### 3.3.3. Mixture Approaches

Supervised learning approaches typically outperform self-supervised methods due to high-quality human annotations. However, self-supervised techniques offer scalable dataset construction without expensive manual labeling. Mixture approaches combine the strengths of both paradigms, providing a practical compromise. Representative works in this category include BLEURT (Sellam et al., [2020](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib334)), FrugalScore (Kamal Eddine et al., [2022](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib135)), and MisMATCH (Murugesan et al., [2023](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib271)). These methods follow a two-stage training process: first pre-training BERT models on large-scale synthetic reference-candidate pairs generated through self-supervised techniques, then fine-tuning on smaller sets of human-annotated scores. This approach leverages the scale of self-supervised data while benefiting from the quality of human annotations, resulting in evaluation metrics that are both robust and cost-effective.

#### 3.3.4. Benchmark-based Evaluation

In addition to automatic evaluation methods, benchmark-based evaluation represents a distinct assessment approach. This method evaluates NLG models by measuring their consistency with human annotations on question-answer pairs, thus assessing the models’ general capabilities. Since human annotation is completed as a one-time effort, subsequent model evaluations only require verifying the match between model-generated answers and human annotations. This verification can be performed through simple answer checking, exact matching, or various automatic evaluation methods.

Table [5](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#S3.T5 "Table 5 ‣ 3.3.4. Benchmark-based Evaluation ‣ 3.3. Learning-based Evaluation ‣ 3. Automatic Evaluation for Neural Text Generation (NLG) ‣ A Survey of Automatic Evaluation Methods on Text, Visual and Speech Generations") provides a complete list of widely used benchmarks for evaluating model capabilities. It can be found that, existing benchmarks can be categorized into six main types (Contributors, [2023](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib46)): (1) Knowledge: Evaluates general and domain-specific knowledge (e.g., C-Eval (Huang et al., [2023a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib122)) for STEM and humanities, MedBench for medical domains) (2) Reasoning: Tests logical capabilities in mathematics (Cobbe et al., [2021](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib42)) and coding tasks (Chen et al., [2021a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib30)) (3) Understanding: Assesses context and query comprehension through benchmarks like MT-Bench101 (Bai et al., [2024](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib11)) and XSum (Narayan et al., [2018](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib272)) (4) Long Text: Measures abilities to handle extended contexts via LongBench (Bai et al., [2023](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib12)) and Ada-LEval (Wang et al., [2024b](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib396)) (5) Agent: Tests automated task solving and function calling capabilities (Chen et al., [2023a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib36); Ma et al., [2024](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib242)) (6) Instruction-Following: Evaluates adherence to instructions and human alignment using metrics like IFEval (Zhou et al., [2023b](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib504))

[TABLE]

Table 5. The list of benchmarks for evaluating the generation models.

### 3.4. LLM-based Evaluation

Learning-based evaluation methods heavily rely on high-quality and diverse training samples, which limits their effectiveness in out-of-domain tasks and their ability to generalize across evaluation criteria. With the development of large language models (LLMs) that demonstrate strong instruction following, context understanding, and zero-shot generalization capabilities, researchers increasingly implement automatic evaluation systems based on these models. This approach is known as LLM-based Evaluation or LLM-as-a-judge (Li et al., [2024b](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib181)).

Compared to previous heuristic, embedding-based, and learning-based automatic evaluation methods, LLM-based evaluation provides not only assessment scores but also textual rationales that analyze flaws in generated content and offer valuable revision suggestions. This results in a more fine-grained and interpretable evaluation paradigm. Current LLM-based evaluation methods fall into two main categories (Li et al., [2024f](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib195)): (1) Prompt-based methods, which incorporate evaluation guidelines in prompts to direct LLMs to function as annotators; (2) Fine-tuning-based methods, which enhance the evaluation capabilities of smaller, more efficient LLMs to address the high computational costs associated with advanced models like GPT-4.

#### 3.4.1. Prompt-based Methods

Through well-designed prompts, LLMs can effectively evaluate various natural language generation (NLG) tasks across different criteria (Fu et al., [2023a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib72); Lin and Chen, [2023](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib208); Es et al., [2024](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib64); Tu et al., [2024c](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib381)). For example, Wang et al. ([2023b](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib403)) and Liu et al. ([2023b](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib223)) demonstrate that GPT-3.5 and GPT-4 perform well as zero-shot evaluators for diverse NLG tasks. Building on these findings, researchers have proposed several techniques to enhance the robustness of LLM evaluation capabilities:

##### (1) Reference:

Zheng et al. ([2023b](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib499)) and Badshah and Sajjad ([2024](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib9)) evaluate response quality by comparing it with reference responses. Recently, BatchEval (Yuan et al., [2024a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib453)) improves LLM-based evaluation by using in-batch examples as references.

##### (2) Criteria:

Qian et al. ([2024](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib300)); Lu et al. ([2024a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib238)) provide detailed evaluation criteria and rubrics to guide LLM assessment processes.

##### (3) Demonstration:

GPTScore (Fu et al., [2023a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib72)), ICE (Jain et al., [2023](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib124)), MSoR (Song et al., [2025b](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib344)), Little Giants (Kotonya et al., [2023](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib149)), and ALLURE (Hasanbeig et al., [2023](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib97)) enhance LLM-based evaluation by incorporating few-shot demonstrations.

##### (4) Swapping:

Swapping operations are widely used to reduce positional bias in pair-wise evaluations (Lan et al., [2024d](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib171); Lee et al., [2024c](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib173); Zheng et al., [2023b](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib499)).

##### (5) Self-consistency:

Zhang et al. ([2024b](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib488)); Zheng et al. ([2023b](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib499)); Manakul et al. ([2023](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib251)); Cohen et al. ([2023](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib43)) show that LLM-as-a-judge performance improves with self-consistency prompting strategies, which sample multiple evaluation rationales and use majority voting for the final result.

##### (6) Multi-Agent:

Addressing single-model bias concerns, researchers have developed multi-agent debate frameworks for evaluation tasks, including ChatEval (Chan et al., [2023b](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib23)), PoLL (Verga et al., [2024](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib387)), and PRD (Li et al., [2024d](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib193)).

##### (7) Claim Decomposition:

Since evaluated responses may contain multiple independent claims, researchers propose decomposing responses into atomic information units (Min et al., [2023](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib260); Sun et al., [2024b](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib354)) and verifying the quality of each unit individually before aggregating results to determine overall response quality.

In summary, most prompt-based methods described above propose prompting strategies to address the inconsistency problem in LLM outputs (Liang et al., [2024](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib199); Zhang et al., [2024b](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib488); Wang et al., [2023d](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib413)). LLMs achieve more accurate judgments when evaluating response quality by following guidelines and clues provided in demonstrations, detailed criteria, and reference responses. Additionally, self-consistency techniques and multi-agent frameworks are widely employed to reduce inconsistencies and biases inherent in single-model evaluations.

Although LLMs can achieve performance comparable to human judgments, their substantial inference costs limit applications in large-scale evaluation scenarios. Recent research has focused on reducing these costs. For example, UniCBE (Yuan et al., [2025a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib454)) introduces a unified uniformity-driven CBE framework that optimizes tuple sampling and preference aggregation strategies in pairwise evaluation protocols. Similarly, TailoredBench (Yuan et al., [2025b](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib456)) proposes a customized evaluation approach tailored to each target model, achieving an average reduction of 31.4% in Mean Absolute Error (MAE) of accuracy estimates under the same inference budget constraints.

#### 3.4.2. Fine-tuning-based Methods

The most direct approach to reduce the cost of LLM-based evaluation is improving the evaluation capabilities of smaller, more efficient models. Many researchers have developed fine-tuning methods to enhance the evaluation performance of small-scale LLMs. These efficient evaluation models address the computational demands of large-scale evaluations, and are widely-used in the recent RLHF training procedure (Mahan et al., [2024](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib247); Lee et al., [2024c](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib173); Ouyang et al., [2022](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib280); DeepSeek-AI et al., [2024](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib55)). So far, current fine-tuning-based LLM evaluation methods can be categorized across four dimensions (Zeng et al., [2024a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib464); Li et al., [2024f](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib195)): (1) Evaluation Interpretability; (2) Evaluation Granularity; (3) Optimization Methods; and (4) Data Sources. Table [6](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#S3.T6 "Table 6 ‣ (4) Data Sources: ‣ 3.4.2. Fine-tuning-based Methods ‣ 3.4. LLM-based Evaluation ‣ 3. Automatic Evaluation for Neural Text Generation (NLG) ‣ A Survey of Automatic Evaluation Methods on Text, Visual and Speech Generations") provides a comprehensive list of these methods, and we describe representative approaches from each dimension below¹¹1Table [6](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#S3.T6 "Table 6 ‣ (4) Data Sources: ‣ 3.4.2. Fine-tuning-based Methods ‣ 3.4. LLM-based Evaluation ‣ 3. Automatic Evaluation for Neural Text Generation (NLG) ‣ A Survey of Automatic Evaluation Methods on Text, Visual and Speech Generations") includes selected representative reward models..

##### (1) Evaluation Interpretability:

Based on interpretability, current fine-tuned LLM-based evaluation models fall into two categories: reward models and critique models. Reward models (Lambert et al., [2024](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib165); Ouyang et al., [2022](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib280)) are trained to mimic human preferences and serve as key components in Reinforcement Learning with Human Feedback (RLHF) (Ouyang et al., [2022](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib280); Stiennon et al., [2020](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib348)). While these models help align LLMs with human preferences, they only provide numerical scores without explanatory feedback, which limits their reliability and interpretability. In contrast, critique models deliver textual analysis of generated content, offering more detailed and interpretable feedback (Tian et al., [2024](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib371); Liu et al., [2024d](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib220)). Recent advances have introduced generative reward models (Ye et al., [2024a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib448); Mahan et al., [2024](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib247)), such as Critic-RM (Yu et al., [2024b](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib452)), which produce chain-of-thought analysis before generating scores. This approach enhances both interpretability and training data efficiency.

##### (2) Evaluation Granularity:

Early evaluation approaches focused on whole-response assessment, known as outcome-based reward models (ORMs) (Lightman et al., [2023b](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib201)). Recent research (Zeng et al., [2024a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib464)) has shown that fine-grained, process reward models (PRMs) offer better transparency and more effective feedback, though they require significant human labor for annotation (Lightman et al., [2023b](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib201)). Most fine-tuning-based evaluation models remain outcome-based, assessing the correctness of entire responses (Cui et al., [2023](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib50); Kim et al., [2024d](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib147)). To improve transparency and explanations, some approaches identify specific flaws in responses and provide detailed analysis, including InstructScore (Xu et al., [2023b](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib439)), TIGERScore (Jiang et al., [2024](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib130)), and MultiCritique (Lan et al., [2024c](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib170)). However, these evaluation signals remain sparse since responses typically contain few flaws.

A growing trend involves providing denser evaluation signals for all intermediate steps in responses through process-based reward models (PRMs). For example, Lightman et al. ([2023b](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib201)) annotate the correctness of each intermediate reasoning step in mathematical solutions. While PRMs offer detailed feedback, annotating intermediate steps is significantly more resource-intensive than evaluating complete responses. To address this challenge, several researchers employ Monte Carlo Tree Search (MCTS) to automatically generate process signals for reasoning tasks with known ground-truth answers, as seen in Math-Shepherd (Wang et al., [2024d](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib404)) and PSRLM (Zhang et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib484)). Nevertheless, most existing PRMs are limited to reasoning tasks with definitive answers, and developing process-based evaluation models for broader domains remains a huge challenging (Zeng et al., [2024a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib464)).

##### (3) Optimization Methods:

Supervised fine-tuning (SFT) represents the most prevalent approach for enhancing LLMs’ evaluation capabilities (Li et al., [2023e](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib188); Cui et al., [2023](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib50)). Notable examples include Auto-J (Li et al., [2023e](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib188)), UltraCM (Cui et al., [2023](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib50)), and Prometheus (Kim et al., [2024c](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib146)), which optimize Llama models using synthetic datasets generated by GPT-4. More recently, researchers have begun exploring preference learning techniques such as Direct Preference Optimization (DPO) (Rafailov et al., [2024](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib305)) and Reinforcement Learning (RL) (Ouyang et al., [2022](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib280)) to overcome the limitations of behavior cloning approaches. Behavior cloning often struggles with distributional shift and fails to capture the nuanced reasoning behind human preferences. Models like Critic-RM (Wang et al., [2024c](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib411)) and SFR-Judge (Wang et al., [2024e](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib405)) demonstrate this shift by optimizing Llama models on preference evaluation datasets, resulting in more robust evaluation capabilities that better align with human judgment patterns.

##### (4) Data Sources:

Similar to the Learning-based evaluation methods described in Section [3.3](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#S3.SS3 "3.3. Learning-based Evaluation ‣ 3. Automatic Evaluation for Neural Text Generation (NLG) ‣ A Survey of Automatic Evaluation Methods on Text, Visual and Speech Generations"), dataset is the core of fine-tuned-based LLM-based evaluation methods. So far, the datasets for optimizing fine-tuned-based evaluation methods originate from three primary sources: (1) Human annotation: This represents the most direct approach to constructing evaluation datasets. For instance, Shepherd (Wang et al., [2023e](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib412)) employs domain experts to annotate textual evaluation samples for training Llama-7B, while CriticGPT (McAleese et al., [2024](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib255)) develops a human-annotated preference critique dataset for training the GPT-4 model. Although human-annotated datasets provide reliable quality, their substantial cost significantly limits scalability, particularly when constructing detailed textual critique datasets. (2) Advanced Teacher Models: Given the considerable resources required for human annotation of textual evaluations, numerous researchers utilize advanced teacher models such as GPT-4 to construct the synthetic dataset for training. Examples include Auto-J (Li et al., [2024b](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib181)) and UltraCM (Cui et al., [2023](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib50)). However, these model-generated datasets often contain significant noise that may compromise the robustness of the resulting evaluation models. Recently, MultiCritique (Lan et al., [2024c](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib170)) proposes to address the noise of one single model by aggregating the diverse critique opinions from multi-agent. (3) Human-machine collaborative annotation: This hybrid approach combines the advantages of both previously mentioned methods. Specifically, LLMs generate draft evaluation samples, which are subsequently validated using human-annotated scores. For example, Themis (Hu et al., [2024b](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib111)) and SFR (Wang et al., [2024e](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib405)) filter high-quality GPT-4 generated samples by measuring the consistency between GPT-4’s judgments and human assessments, resulting in more reliable training data while maintaining cost efficiency. This approach could leverage simple human-annotated evaluation scores or preferences as ground-truth to collect reliable detailed textual critiques, thereby avoiding the substantial expense of annotating detailed critiques from scratch.

[TABLE]

Table 6. The list of the fine-tuning-based LLM-based evaluation models. As for the Data Source, Human-Model indicates that the training data are generated by using LLMs and verified by human annotations. The human annotations could be reference answers in reasoning tasks (Tang et al., [2025a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib366)) or human-annotated preference labels (Wang et al., [2024e](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib405); Hu et al., [2024b](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib111); Yu et al., [2024b](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib452)).

### 3.5. Comparing Automatic Evaluations

In this section, we present both qualitative and quantitative analyses of existing automatic evaluation paradigms for text generation.

#### 3.5.1. Qualitative Analysis

Table [7](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#S3.T7 "Table 7 ‣ 3.5.1. Qualitative Analysis ‣ 3.5. Comparing Automatic Evaluations ‣ 3. Automatic Evaluation for Neural Text Generation (NLG) ‣ A Survey of Automatic Evaluation Methods on Text, Visual and Speech Generations") summarizes our comparative analysis of existing automatic evaluation methods across four key dimensions: (1) Evaluation Flexibility and Generalization, which examines the adaptability of methods across different tasks and evaluation criteria; (2) Training Data Source, which identifies the origin and nature of data used to develop these methods; (3) Evaluation Granularity, which considers the level of detail in the assessment process; and (4) Evaluation Cost, which accounts for computational resources and time requirements. These dimensions provide a comprehensive framework for understanding the strengths and limitations of current automatic evaluation approaches.

[TABLE]

Table 7. The comparison results of five evaluation paradigm on six dimensions. Scalar denotes the scalar-based evaluation outcome, such as scores and preference labels. Process-level indicates recent process-based reward models (Zeng et al., [2024a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib464)) that evaluates the quality of each process in the generation.

##### Evaluation Flexibility and Generalization

The evolution of NLG evaluation techniques shows a clear shift from task-specific methods toward more generalizable approaches. Early techniques (heuristic, embedding-based, and learning-based) were limited to specific tasks and criteria (Fu et al., [2023a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib72)). In contrast, modern LLM-based methods achieve remarkable flexibility through simple prompt engineering, enabling general evaluation across diverse tasks and customized criteria (Liu et al., [2024h](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib226)).

Recently, fine-tuned LLM-based automatic evaluation methods have emerged as alternatives to resource-intensive prompt-based approaches. However, these trained evaluation methods often sacrifice generalization capability, particularly for tasks and evaluation criteria not covered in their training data (Lan et al., [2024c](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib170)). The explicit learning of evaluation criteria represents a crucial direction for future development. Recent work has begun exploring this area. For example, HD-Eval (Liu et al., [2024h](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib226)) learns to construct a hierarchical criteria tree using human-annotation labels and demonstrates that this criteria structure improves LLM-based evaluation methods like G-Eval (Liu et al., [2023b](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib223)). Similarly, MultiCritique (Lan et al., [2024c](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib170)) jointly learns customized criteria and evaluation generation within a multi-task learning framework, significantly enhancing evaluation generalization.

##### Training Data Source

The reliability of learning-based or fine-tuning-based (LLM-based) automatic evaluation methods heavily depends on training data quality. As shown in Table [7](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#S3.T7 "Table 7 ‣ 3.5.1. Qualitative Analysis ‣ 3.5. Comparing Automatic Evaluations ‣ 3. Automatic Evaluation for Neural Text Generation (NLG) ‣ A Survey of Automatic Evaluation Methods on Text, Visual and Speech Generations"), training data sources come from either human annotation or synthetic data generation (Wang et al., [2023e](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib412); Ye et al., [2024a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib448); Hu et al., [2024b](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib111)). Human annotation provides precise labels but incurs substantial costs that prohibit scaling. This limitation is especially significant for recent interpretable fine-tuning-based automatic evaluation methods. Synthetic data offers a cost-efficient alternative but suffers from quality limitations inherent to the generative models’ capabilities. To address this challenge, recent approaches have developed human-machine collaborative annotation method. Methods like SCRIT (Tang et al., [2025a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib366)) and Critic-RM (Yu et al., [2024b](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib452)) validate synthetic data with human-annotated labels, creating more efficient and scalable solutions to improve evaluation capability. As task complexity increases, a significant challenge emerges: neither teacher models nor human experts alone can provide sufficiently reliable supervision. Future research should focus on developing methods to accurately supervise generations for challenging tasks, such as complex reasoning problems or open-ended questions.

##### Evaluation Granularity

The evolution of evaluation techniques has led to increasingly fine-grained assessment capabilities. Traditional methods (heuristic, embedding-based, and learning-based) employ outcome-based evaluation (Zeng et al., [2024a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib464)), providing holistic quality scores or binary preference labels without detailed justification. In contrast, LLM-based evaluation methods leverage their advanced understanding and generation capabilities to deliver multi-dimensional assessments. These methods not only produce quantitative metrics such as numerical scores or preference rankings, but also provide fine-grained, explainable evaluation rationales (Liu et al., [2023b](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib223)). This explanatory capability operates at both process and step levels—examining the reasoning path and individual decision points rather than just the final output.

##### Evaluation Cost

While evaluation techniques have become more advanced and robust, their computational cost has also increased dramatically, especially for recent LLM-based evaluation methods. Large foundation models like GPT-4 require significant computational resources, making large-scale evaluations prohibitively expensive and time-consuming for many research teams and organizations.

To address this efficiency challenge, fine-tuning-based evaluation methods have emerged as a promising solution within the LLM-based evaluation paradigm. These approaches aim to distill the sophisticated evaluation capabilities of large-scale LLMs like GPT-4 into smaller, more efficient models. The resulting compact evaluators significantly reduce inference time and computational requirements while maintaining comparable evaluation quality. These smaller evaluation models offer two key advantages: First, they enable efficient large-scale evaluations that would be cost-prohibitive with larger models. Second, they can serve as more robust and reliable reward models in Reinforcement Learning from Human Feedback (RLHF) training pipelines (DeepSeek-AI et al., [2024](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib55); Ye et al., [2024a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib448)), where repeated evaluations are required during the optimization process. This balance between evaluation quality and computational efficiency represents an important direction for making advanced evaluation techniques more accessible and practical.

#### 3.5.2. Quantitative Analysis

This section systematically examines performance differences among representative works across various automatic evaluation methods through comparative experiments, extending beyond qualitative analysis. Before presenting our quantitative results, we introduce the concept of meta-evaluation—the process of evaluating automatic evaluation methods.

##### Meta-Evaluation

Meta-evaluation aims to evaluate the reliability of the automatic evaluation. So far, the meta-evaluation could be classified into two categories: objective evaluation and subjective evaluation.

Meta-evaluation assesses the reliability of automatic evaluation methods, i.e., whether the automatic evaluation correlates with human judgments. Current meta-evaluation approaches fall into two main categories: objective and subjective evaluation (Lan et al., [2024d](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib171)).

(1) Objective Evaluation: Objective meta-evaluation methods fall into the following categories:

- •
  Single-wise evaluation protocol measures correlation between automatic evaluation methods and human judgments. Spearman, Pearson, and Kendall correlation coefficients are widely used in established meta-evaluation benchmarks such as SummEval (Fabbri et al., [2021](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib65)), FED (Mehri and Eskenazi, [2020a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib256)), and OpenMEVA (Guan et al., [2021](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib89)).
- •
  Pair-wise evaluation protocol measures agreement (accuracy) between model-generated preferences $`p`$ and human-annotated preferences, like RewardBench (Lambert et al., [2024](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib165)) and Auto-J (Li et al., [2023e](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib188)).
- •
  Corpus-wise evaluation protocol calculates correlation between quality judgments from automatic evaluation methods and human annotators, where a quality judgment means choosing a particular (generative model and decoder) setting (Pillutla et al., [2021](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib295)).

(2) Subjective Evaluation: Subjective meta-evaluation methods primarily assess the quality of evaluation rationales. Recent studies employ GPT-4 as a judge to score rationale quality (Li et al., [2024e](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib187); Cui et al., [2023](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib50); Wang et al., [2023e](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib412); Kim et al., [2024b](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib144)). However, due to the complexity of automatic evaluation tasks, even GPT-4 cannot consistently provide reliable subjective meta-evaluation (Wang et al., [2023e](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib412); McAleese et al., [2024](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib255)). Recent work by CriticEval (Lan et al., [2024d](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib171)) and MetaCritique (Sun et al., [2024b](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib354)) shows that GPT-4 can achieve reliable meta-evaluation when human-annotated evaluation rationales are provided as reference evaluation rationale (critiques).

Numerous meta-evaluation benchmarks have been proposed to measure the reliability of automatic evaluations. As described in Table [8](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#S3.T8 "Table 8 ‣ Meta-Evaluation ‣ 3.5.2. Quantitative Analysis ‣ 3.5. Comparing Automatic Evaluations ‣ 3. Automatic Evaluation for Neural Text Generation (NLG) ‣ A Survey of Automatic Evaluation Methods on Text, Visual and Speech Generations"), early meta-evaluation benchmarks primarily focused on specific NLG tasks, including machine translation (Freitag et al., [2022](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib71); Fu et al., [2023a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib72)), text summarization (Fabbri et al., [2021](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib65); Bhandari et al., [2020](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib15); Grusky et al., [2018](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib86)), data-to-text generation (Mairesse et al., [2010](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib249); Wen et al., [2015](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib421); Zhou and Lampouras, [2020](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib503); Lin et al., [2020](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib202)), dialog generation (Mehri and Eskenazi, [2020a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib256), [b](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib257)), and story generation (Guan et al., [2021](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib89); Wang et al., [2022](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib408)). With the advanced generation and generalization capabilities of LLMs, researchers have developed systematic general-domain meta-evaluation benchmarks to assess their evaluation capabilities, such as MT-Bench, Chat-Arena (Zheng et al., [2023a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib498)), RewardBench (Lambert et al., [2024](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib165)), and RM-Bench (Liu et al., [2024i](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib227)). Additionally, specialized meta-evaluation benchmarks measure LLMs’ evaluation performance on challenging tasks, including reasoning (Luo et al., [2023](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib240); Lin et al., [2024b](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib210); Tan et al., [2024](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib364)), safety alignment (Yuan et al., [2024b](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib457)), and information retrieval (Lu et al., [2024a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib238)).

[TABLE]

Table 8. The widely used meta-evaluation benchmarks for NLG tasks. MT, SM, D2T, Dialog, Reason, SG, EE and Safety represent the machine translation, text summarization, data-to-text, dialogue generation, logic reasoning, story generation, event extraction and safety NLG tasks. General task type indicate that the meta-evaluation benchmarks cover multiple diverse domains.

##### Systematic Meta-evaluation Results

[TABLE]

Table 9. Performance (Spearman correlation scores) of representative automatic evaluation methods on 7 NLG meta-evaluation benchmarks. Partial results remain empty due to the incomplete evaluations in previous studies. Due to the limited work on Embedding-based - Divergence methods, results are not feasible.

[TABLE]

Table 10. Performance of LLM-based evaluation methods on 5 meta-evaluation benchmarks on general domains. Metric for CriticEval-single is the Spearman correlation, and metrics for other five meta-benchmarks are preference accuracy.

We performed systematic comparisons and evaluations of representative works from four automatic evaluation approaches (heuristic, embedding-based, learning-based, and LLM-based methods²²2Benchmark-based evaluation methods do not require meta-evaluation.) across 12 key meta-evaluation benchmarks: (1) SummEval (Fabbri et al., [2021](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib65)) for text summarization; (2) Topical-Chat (Mehri and Eskenazi, [2020b](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib257)) for personalized dialogue generation; (3) FED (Mehri and Eskenazi, [2020a](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib256)) for general open-domain dialogue generation; (4) WMT22 (Freitag et al., [2022](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib71)) for machine translation; (5) OpenMEVA (Guan et al., [2021](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib89)) for story generation; (6) BAGEL (Mairesse et al., [2010](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib249)) for data-to-text generation; (7) WebNLG (Zhou and Lampouras, [2020](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib503)) for data-to-text generation; (8) CriticBench (Lin et al., [2024b](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib210)) for reasoning tasks; (9) CriticEval (Lan et al., [2024d](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib171)) for 9 diverse NLG tasks on single-wise (CriticEval-single) and pair-wise (CriticEval-pair) evaluation protocols; (10) Auto-J (Li et al., [2024b](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib181)) for 58 diverse NLG tasks; (11) PreferenceBench (Kim et al., [2024c](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib146)) for diverse NLG tasks; and (12) RewardBench (Lambert et al., [2024](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib165)) for evaluating reward models across chat, safty and reasoning tasks. Please refer to more details about these meta-evaluation benchmarks in Table [8](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#S3.T8 "Table 8 ‣ Meta-Evaluation ‣ 3.5.2. Quantitative Analysis ‣ 3.5. Comparing Automatic Evaluations ‣ 3. Automatic Evaluation for Neural Text Generation (NLG) ‣ A Survey of Automatic Evaluation Methods on Text, Visual and Speech Generations").

Since the last five meta-evaluation benchmarks span diverse domains, we only tested representative LLM-based evaluation methods, as heuristic, embedding-based, and learning-based evaluation approaches cannot be effectively evaluated across these domains. Based on the results presented in Table [9](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#S3.T9 "Table 9 ‣ Systematic Meta-evaluation Results ‣ 3.5.2. Quantitative Analysis ‣ 3.5. Comparing Automatic Evaluations ‣ 3. Automatic Evaluation for Neural Text Generation (NLG) ‣ A Survey of Automatic Evaluation Methods on Text, Visual and Speech Generations") and Table [10](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#S3.T10 "Table 10 ‣ Systematic Meta-evaluation Results ‣ 3.5.2. Quantitative Analysis ‣ 3.5. Comparing Automatic Evaluations ‣ 3. Automatic Evaluation for Neural Text Generation (NLG) ‣ A Survey of Automatic Evaluation Methods on Text, Visual and Speech Generations"), we can draw several important conclusions:

- •
  LLM-based automated evaluation methods currently outperform other approaches significantly. Across the seven benchmarks, LLM-based methods are only slightly less effective than COMET-22 (Rei et al., [2020](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#bib.bib312)) on the WMT-22 benchmark for machine translation tasks.
- •
  Fine-tuned LLM-based evaluation methods also substantially outperform heuristic, embedding-based, and learning-based methods. This demonstrates that distilling evaluation capabilities from strong LLMs can produce efficient, high-quality automated evaluation models.
- •
  Table [10](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#S3.T10 "Table 10 ‣ Systematic Meta-evaluation Results ‣ 3.5.2. Quantitative Analysis ‣ 3.5. Comparing Automatic Evaluations ‣ 3. Automatic Evaluation for Neural Text Generation (NLG) ‣ A Survey of Automatic Evaluation Methods on Text, Visual and Speech Generations") shows that reward models fine-tuned on human-annotated preference datasets outperform interpretable critique models and even surpass prompt-based methods on several benchmarks (e.g., Auto-J and RewardBench). This highlights the effectiveness of human-annotated training data for evaluation tasks. However, Table [9](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#S3.T9 "Table 9 ‣ Systematic Meta-evaluation Results ‣ 3.5.2. Quantitative Analysis ‣ 3.5. Comparing Automatic Evaluations ‣ 3. Automatic Evaluation for Neural Text Generation (NLG) ‣ A Survey of Automatic Evaluation Methods on Text, Visual and Speech Generations") reveals that reward models significantly underperform compared to prompt-based methods on seven meta-evaluation benchmarks for specific NLG tasks. This finding indicates that generalization capability remains a major limitation of reward models. Future work should prioritize enhancing the generalization ability of these models.
- •
  Recent reasoning models optimized for mathematical and coding questions, such as OpenAI o1 and DeepSeek-R1, demonstrate strong critique abilities when solving complex problems. This raises an important question: Are reasoning models more suitable for evaluation than LLMs? As shown in Tables [9](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#S3.T9 "Table 9 ‣ Systematic Meta-evaluation Results ‣ 3.5.2. Quantitative Analysis ‣ 3.5. Comparing Automatic Evaluations ‣ 3. Automatic Evaluation for Neural Text Generation (NLG) ‣ A Survey of Automatic Evaluation Methods on Text, Visual and Speech Generations") and [10](https://arxiv.org/html/2506.10019v1#S3.T10 "Table 10 ‣ Systematic Meta-evaluation Results ‣ 3.5.2. Quantitative Analysis ‣ 3.5. Comparing Automatic Evaluations ‣ 3. Automatic Evaluation for Neural Text Generation (NLG) ‣ A Survey of Automatic Evaluation Methods on Text, Visual and Speech Generations"), the state-of-the-art reasoning model DeepSeek-R1 outperforms its base model DeepSeek-V3 and GPT-4 only on specific benchmarks like CriticBench and CriticEval-Pair. This indicates that reasoning models are not universally superior to traditional LLM evaluation methods. DeepSeek-R1 performs exceptionally well on CriticBench, which includes diverse meta-evaluation tasks focused on complex reasoning, suggesting it may be particularly suitable for evaluating generation quality in complex reasoning tasks.
